   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country on the west coast of Central Africa
      </div>

      </div>

      <p><b>Gabon</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>French pronunciation:&nbsp;​</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[ɡabɔ̃]</a></span>), officially the <b>Gabonese Republic</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">République gabonaise</i>), is a country on the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>. Located on the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, Gabon is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a> on the east and south, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a> to the west. It has an area of nearly 270,000 square kilometres (100,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) and its population is estimated at 2.1 million people. There are three distinct regions: the coastal plains, the mountains (the Cristal Mountains and the Chaillu Massif in the centre), and the <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> in the east. The coastal plains form part of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Equatorial_coastal_forests" title="Atlantic Equatorial coastal forests">Atlantic Equatorial coastal forests</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a>. Gabon's capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Libreville" title="Libreville">Libreville</a>. The official language is <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>. </p>
      <p>Originally settled by <a href="/wiki/Congo_Pygmies" class="mw-redirect" title="Congo Pygmies">Pygmy peoples</a>, they were largely replaced and absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> tribes as <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">they migrated</a>. By the 18th century, a <a href="/wiki/Myene_language" title="Myene language">Myeni</a>-speaking kingdom known as the <a href="/wiki/Orungu_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Orungu Kingdom">Kingdom of Orungu</a> formed in Gabon. It was able to become a powerful trading center mainly due to its ability to purchase and sell slaves. The kingdom fell with the demise of the slave trade in the 1870s. Since its independence from France in 1960, the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> of Gabon has had three <a href="/wiki/President_of_Gabon" title="President of Gabon">presidents</a>. In the early 1990s, Gabon introduced a multi-party system and a new democratic constitution that allowed for a more transparent electoral process and reformed many governmental institutions. </p>
      <p>Abundant petroleum and foreign private investment have helped make Gabon one of the most prosperous countries in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of African countries by Human Development Index">fifth highest</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">HDI</a><sup id="cite_ref-HDI_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-7">[7]</a></sup> in the region (after <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of African countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">fifth highest</a> GDP per capita (PPP) in all of Africa (after Seychelles, Mauritius, <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a> and Botswana). Its GDP grew by more than 6% per year from 2010 to 2012. However, because of <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">inequality</a> in income distribution, a significant proportion of the population remains poor. </p>
      <p>Gabon is rich in <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Mythology">mythology</a>. "Raconteurs" keep traditions alive such as the mvett among the Fangs and the ingwala among the Nzebis. Gabon is also known for it masks, such as the n'goltang (Fang) and the reliquary figures of the <a href="/wiki/Kota_people_(Gabon)" title="Kota people (Gabon)">Kota</a>. Musically, Gabon boasts an array of folk styles, as well as pop stars like <a href="/wiki/Patience_Dabany" title="Patience Dabany">Patience Dabany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Annie-Flore_Batchiellilys" title="Annie-Flore Batchiellilys">Annie-Flore Batchiellilys</a>. Also known are guitarists like Georges Oyendze, La Rose Mbadou and Sylvain Avara, and the singer <a href="/wiki/Oliver_N%27Goma" title="Oliver N'Goma">Oliver N'Goma</a>. Gabonese folk instruments include the obala, the <a href="/wiki/Ngombi" title="Ngombi">ngombi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balafon" title="Balafon">balafon</a> and traditional drums. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Gabon's name originates from <i>gabão</i>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> for "cloak", which is roughly the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Komo_River" title="Komo River">Komo River</a> by Libreville. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Gabon" title="History of Gabon">History of Gabon</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Equatorial_Africa" title="French Equatorial Africa">French Equatorial Africa</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_(_Gold_Coast,_Slave_Coast,_Ivory_Coast_)_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg/220px-1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg/330px-1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg/440px-1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_%28_Gold_Coast%2C_Slave_Coast%2C_Ivory_Coast_%29_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="2591"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:1670_Ogilby_Map_of_West_Africa_(_Gold_Coast,_Slave_Coast,_Ivory_Coast_)_-_Geographicus_-_Guinea-ogilby-1670.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A map of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> in 1670
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span id="Pre-Colonial_Era_.28pre-1885.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Colonial_Era_(pre-1885)">Pre-Colonial Era (pre-1885)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-Colonial Era (pre-1885)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The earliest inhabitants of the area were <a href="/wiki/Congo_Pygmies" class="mw-redirect" title="Congo Pygmies">Pygmy peoples</a>. They were largely replaced and absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> tribes as <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">they migrated</a>. </p>
      <p>In the 15th century, the first Europeans arrived. By the 18th century, a <a href="/wiki/Myene_language" title="Myene language">Myeni</a>-speaking kingdom known as <a href="/wiki/Orungu_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Orungu Kingdom">Orungu</a> formed in Gabon. Through its control of the slave trade in the 18th and 19th centuries, it was able to become the most powerful of the trading centers that developed in Gabon during that period. </p>
      <p>On February 10, 1722, <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Roberts" title="Bartholomew Roberts">Bartholomew Roberts</a>, Barti Ddu, a Welsh pirate known in English as Black Bart, died at sea off <a href="/wiki/Cape_Lopez" title="Cape Lopez">Cape Lopez</a>. He raided ships off the Americas and West Africa from 1719 to 1722. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Colonial_Era_.281885.E2.80.931960.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Era_(1885–1960)">Colonial Era (1885–1960)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial Era (1885–1960)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>French explorer <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza" title="Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza">Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza</a> led his first mission to the Gabon-Congo area in 1875. He founded the town of <a href="/wiki/Franceville" title="Franceville">Franceville</a>, and was later colonial governor. Several Bantu groups lived in the area that is now Gabon when France officially occupied it in 1885. </p>
      <p>In 1910, Gabon became one of the four territories of <a href="/wiki/French_Equatorial_Africa" title="French Equatorial Africa">French Equatorial Africa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> a federation that survived until 1958. In <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the Allies <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gabon" title="Battle of Gabon">invaded</a> Gabon in order to overthrow the pro-<a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a> colonial administration. </p>
      <p>On November 28, 1958, Gabon became an autonomous republic within the <a href="/wiki/French_Community" title="French Community">French Community</a>, and on August 17, 1960, it became fully independent.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Post-Independence_.281960.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Independence_(1960–present)">Post-Independence (1960–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Post-Independence (1960–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The first president of Gabon, elected in 1961, was <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_M%27ba" title="Léon M'ba">Léon M'ba</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo_Ondimba" class="mw-redirect" title="Omar Bongo Ondimba">Omar Bongo Ondimba</a> as his vice president. </p>
      <p>After M'ba's accession to power, the press was suppressed, political demonstrations banned, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a> curtailed, other political parties gradually excluded from power, and the Constitution changed along French lines to vest power in the Presidency, a post that M'ba assumed himself. However, when M'ba dissolved the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Gabon)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly (Gabon)">National Assembly</a> in January 1964 to institute one-party rule, <a href="/wiki/1964_Gabonese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1964 Gabonese coup d'état">an army coup sought to oust him from power</a> and restore parliamentary democracy. French paratroopers flew in within 24 hours to restore M'ba to power. </p>
      <p>After a few days of fighting, the coup ended and the opposition was imprisoned, despite widespread protests and riots. French soldiers still remain in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Camp_de_Gaulle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Camp de Gaulle (page does not exist)">Camp de Gaulle</a> on the outskirts of Gabon's capital to this day. When M'Ba died in 1967, Bongo replaced him as president. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg/220px-Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg/330px-Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg/440px-Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1531" data-file-height="1220"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_campaign_%271e_Compagnie_de_Chars_de_Combat_de_la_France_Libre%27.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gabon" title="Battle of Gabon">Battle of Gabon</a> resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French Forces">Free French Forces</a> taking the colony of Gabon from <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy French</a> forces, 1940
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In March 1968, Bongo declared Gabon a one-party state by dissolving the BDG and establishing a new party—the <a href="/wiki/Parti_Democratique_Gabonais" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti Democratique Gabonais">Parti Democratique Gabonais</a> (PDG). He invited all Gabonese, regardless of previous political affiliation, to participate. Bongo sought to forge a single national movement in support of the government's development policies, using the PDG as a tool to submerge the regional and tribal rivalries that had divided Gabonese politics in the past. Bongo was elected president in February 1975; in April 1975, the position of vice president was abolished and replaced by the position of prime minister, who had no right to automatic succession. Bongo was re-elected President in both December 1979 and November 1986 to 7-year terms.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In early 1990 economic discontent and a desire for political liberalization provoked violent demonstrations and strikes by students and workers. In response to grievances by workers, Bongo negotiated with them on a sector-by-sector basis, making significant wage concessions. In addition, he promised to open up the PDG and to organize a national political conference in March–April 1990 to discuss Gabon's future political system. The PDG and 74 political organizations attended the conference. Participants essentially divided into two loose coalitions, the ruling PDG and its allies, and the United Front of Opposition Associations and Parties, consisting of the breakaway Morena Fundamental and the <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Progress_Party" title="Gabonese Progress Party">Gabonese Progress Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The April 1990 conference approved sweeping political reforms, including creation of a national <a href="/wiki/Senate_(Gabon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate (Gabon)">Senate</a>, decentralization of the budgetary process, freedom of assembly and press, and cancellation of an <a href="/wiki/Exit_visa" class="mw-redirect" title="Exit visa">exit visa</a> requirement. In an attempt to guide the political system's transformation to multiparty democracy, Bongo resigned as PDG chairman and created a transitional government headed by a new Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Casimir_Oye-Mba" class="mw-redirect" title="Casimir Oye-Mba">Casimir Oye-Mba</a>. The Gabonese Social Democratic Grouping (RSDG), as the resulting government was called, was smaller than the previous government and included representatives from several opposition parties in its cabinet. The RSDG drafted a provisional constitution in May 1990 that provided a basic <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a> and an independent judiciary but retained strong executive powers for the president. After further review by a constitutional committee and the National Assembly, this document came into force in March 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Opposition to the PDG continued after the April 1990 conference, however, and in September 1990, two <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> attempts were uncovered and aborted. Despite anti-government demonstrations after the untimely death of an opposition leader, the first multiparty National Assembly elections in almost 30 years took place in September–October 1990, with the PDG garnering a large majority.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg/220px-Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg/330px-Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg/440px-Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="514" data-file-height="355"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Omar_Bongo_with_George_Bush_May_26_2004-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> welcomes President <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo" title="Omar Bongo">Omar Bongo</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>, May 2004
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following President <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo" title="Omar Bongo">Omar Bongo</a>'s re-election in December 1993 with 51% of the vote, opposition candidates refused to validate the election results. Serious civil disturbances and violent repression led to an agreement between the government and opposition factions to work toward a political settlement. These talks led to the Paris Accords in November 1994, under which several opposition figures were included in a government of national unity. This arrangement soon broke down, however, and the 1996 and 1997 legislative and municipal elections provided the background for renewed partisan politics. The PDG won a landslide victory in the legislative election, but several major cities, including <a href="/wiki/Libreville" title="Libreville">Libreville</a>, elected opposition mayors during the 1997 local election.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Facing a divided opposition, President Omar Bongo coasted to easy re-election in December 1998, with large majorities of the vote. While Bongo's major opponents rejected the outcome as fraudulent, some international observers characterized the results as representative despite many perceived irregularities, and there were none of the civil disturbances that followed the 1993 election. Peaceful though flawed legislative elections held in 2001–2002, which were boycotted by a number of smaller opposition parties and were widely criticized for their administrative weaknesses, produced a National Assembly almost completely dominated by the PDG and allied independents. In November 2005 President Omar Bongo was elected for his sixth term. He won re-election easily, but opponents claim that the balloting process was marred by irregularities. There were some instances of violence following the announcement of his win, but Gabon generally remained peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>National Assembly elections were held again in December 2006. Several seats contested because of voting irregularities were overturned by the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_Gabon" title="Constitutional Court of Gabon">Constitutional Court</a>, but the subsequent run-off elections in early 2007 again yielded a PDG-controlled National Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG/220px-Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG/330px-Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG/440px-Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Celebration_in_Gabon.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Independence Day celebration in Gabon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On June 8, 2009, President Omar Bongo died of cardiac arrest at a Spanish hospital in Barcelona, ushering in a new era in Gabonese politics. In accordance with the amended constitution, <a href="/wiki/Rose_Francine_Rogomb%C3%A9" title="Rose Francine Rogombé">Rose Francine Rogombé</a>, the President of the Senate, became Interim President on June 10, 2009. The first contested elections in Gabon's history that did not include Omar Bongo as a candidate were held on August 30, 2009 with 18 candidates for president. The lead-up to the elections saw some isolated protests, but no significant disturbances. Omar Bongo's son, ruling party leader <a href="/wiki/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba" title="Ali Bongo Ondimba">Ali Bongo Ondimba</a>, was formally declared the winner after a 3-week review by the Constitutional Court; his inauguration took place on October 16, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The court's review had been prompted by claims of fraud by the many opposition candidates, with the initial announcement of election results sparking unprecedented violent protests in <a href="/wiki/Port-Gentil" title="Port-Gentil">Port-Gentil</a>, the country's second-largest city and a long-time bastion of opposition to PDG rule. The citizens of Port-Gentil took to the streets, and numerous shops and residences were burned, including the French Consulate and a local prison. Officially, only four deaths occurred during the riots, but opposition and local leaders claim many more. Gendarmes and the military were deployed to Port-Gentil to support the beleaguered police, and a curfew was in effect for more than three months.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A partial legislative by-election was held in June 2010. A newly created coalition of parties, the Union Nationale (UN), participated for the first time. The UN is composed largely of PDG defectors who left the party after <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo" title="Omar Bongo">Omar Bongo</a>'s death. Of the five hotly contested seats, the PDG won three and the UN won two; both sides claimed victory.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In January 2019, there was an <a href="/wiki/2019_Gabonese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="2019 Gabonese coup d'état attempt">attempted coup d'état</a> led by soldiers against the President <a href="/wiki/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba" title="Ali Bongo Ondimba">Ali Bongo</a>; the coup ultimately failed.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Gabon" title="Politics of Gabon">Politics of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg/220px-Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg/330px-Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg/440px-Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3960" data-file-height="2640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ali_Bongo_Ondimba_with_Obamas_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Ali_Bongo_Ondimba" title="Ali Bongo Ondimba">Ali Bongo Ondimba</a>, President of the Gabonese Republic, his wife Sylvia Bongo Ondimba, US president <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> in 2014
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon is a republic with a presidential form of government under the 1961 constitution (revised in 1975, rewritten in 1991, and revised in 2003). The president is elected by universal suffrage for a seven-year term; a 2003 constitutional amendment removed presidential term limits and facilitated a presidency for life. The president can appoint and dismiss the prime minister, the cabinet, and judges of the independent Supreme Court. The president also has other strong powers, such as authority to dissolve the National Assembly, declare a state of siege, delay legislation, and conduct referenda.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gabon has a <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Gabon" title="Parliament of Gabon">bicameral legislature</a> with a National Assembly and Senate. The National Assembly has 120 deputies who are popularly elected for a 5-year term. The Senate is composed of 102 members who are elected by municipal councils and regional assemblies and serve for 6 years. The Senate was created in the 1990–1991 constitutional revision, although it was not brought into being until after the 1997 local elections. The President of the Senate is next in succession to the President.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Despite the democratic system of government, the <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a></i> report lists Gabon as "not free", and elections in 2016 have been disputed. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">Political culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Political culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 1990, the government made major changes to Gabon's political system. A transitional constitution was drafted in May 1990 as an outgrowth of the national political conference in March–April and later revised by a constitutional committee. Among its provisions were a Western-style bill of rights, creation of a National Council of Democracy to oversee the guarantee of those rights, a governmental advisory board on economic and social issues, and an independent judiciary.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After approval by the National Assembly, the PDG Central Committee, and the President, the Assembly unanimously adopted the constitution in March 1991. Multiparty legislative elections were held in 1990–91, despite the fact that opposition parties had not been declared formally legal. In spite of this, the elections produced the first representative, multiparty National Assembly. In January 1991, the Assembly passed by unanimous vote a law governing the legalization of opposition parties.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After President Omar Bongo was re-elected in 1993, in a disputed election where only 51% of votes were cast, social and political disturbances led to the 1994 Paris Conference and Accords. These provided a framework for the next elections. Local and legislative elections were delayed until 1996–97. In 1997, constitutional amendments put forward years earlier were adopted to create the Senate and the position of vice president, as well as to extend the president's term to seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In October 2009, newly elected President Ali Bongo Ondimba began efforts to streamline the government. In an effort to reduce corruption and government bloat, he eliminated 17 minister-level positions, abolished the vice presidency and reorganized the portfolios of numerous ministries, bureaus and directorates. In November 2009, President Bongo Ondimba announced a new vision for the modernization of Gabon, called "Gabon Emergent". This program contains three pillars: Green Gabon, Service Gabon, and Industrial Gabon. The goals of Gabon Emergent are to diversify the economy so that Gabon becomes less reliant on petroleum, to eliminate corruption, and to modernize the workforce. Under this program, exports of raw timber have been banned, a government-wide census was held, the work day has been changed to eliminate a long midday break, and a national oil company was created.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In provisional results,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2012)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> the ruling <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Democratic_Party" title="Gabonese Democratic Party">Gabonese Democratic Party</a> (PDG) won 84 out of 120 parliamentary seats. </p>
      <p>On January 25, 2011, opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Mba_Obame" title="André Mba Obame">André Mba Obame</a> claimed the presidency, saying the country should be run by someone the people really wanted. He also selected 19 ministers for his government, and the entire group, along with hundreds of others, spent the night at UN headquarters. On January 26, the government dissolved Mba Obame's party. AU chairman <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ping" title="Jean Ping">Jean Ping</a> said that Mba Obame's action "hurts the integrity of legitimate institutions and also endangers the peace, the security and the stability of Gabon."<sup id="cite_ref-Goma_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goma-12">[12]</a></sup> Interior Minister Jean-François Ndongou accused Mba Obame and his supporters of <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goma_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goma-12">[12]</a></sup> The UN Secretary-General, <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a>, said that he recognized Ondimba as the only official Gabonese president.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (February 2020)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_presidential_election,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="Gabonese presidential election, 2016">2016 presidential election</a> was disputed, with very close official results reported. Protests broke out in the capital and met a brutal repression which culminated in the alleged bombing of opposition party headquarters by the presidential guard. Between 50 and 100 citizens were killed by security forces and 1,000 arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> International observers criticized irregularities, including unnaturally high turnout reported for some districts. The country's supreme court threw out some suspect precincts, but a full recount was not possible because ballots had been destroyed. The election was declared in favor of the incumbent Ondimba. European Parliament issued 2 resolutions denouncing the unclear results of the election and calling for an independent investigation on the human rights violations.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Gabon" title="Foreign relations of Gabon">Foreign relations of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg/220px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg/440px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1880" data-file-height="1160"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_with_Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale_%26_his_spouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Prime Minister of Gabon <a href="/wiki/Julien_Nkoghe_Bekale" title="Julien Nkoghe Bekale">Julien Nkoghe Bekale</a> and Russian President <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Russia-Africa_Summit" title="Russia-Africa Summit">Russia-Africa Summit</a> in Sochi in October 2019
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Since independence, Gabon has followed a nonaligned policy, advocating dialogue in international affairs and recognizing each side of divided countries. In inter-African affairs, Gabon espouses development by evolution rather than revolution and favors regulated private enterprise as the system most likely to promote rapid economic growth. Gabon played an important leadership role in the stability of Central Africa through involvement in mediation efforts in <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> (D.R.C.), and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>. </p>
      <p>In December 1999, through the mediation efforts of President Bongo, a peace accord was signed in the Republic of the Congo (Brazzaville) between the government and most leaders of an armed rebellion. President Bongo was also involved in the continuing D.R.C. peace process, and played a role in mediating the crisis in <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>. Gabonese armed forces were also an integral part of the Central African Economic and Monetary Community (CEMAC) mission to the Central African Republic. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_080107-N-4044H-049_Capt._John_B._Nowell_Jr.,_commodore,_Africa_Partnership_Station_(APS)_is_greeted_by_Gabonese_Army_as_he_arrives_at_a_scheduled_meeting_with_General_Ntori_Longo,_chief_of_Gabonese_Armed_Services,_about.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/US_Navy_080107-N-4044H-049_Capt._John_B._Nowell_Jr.%2C_commodore%2C_Africa_Partnership_Station_%28APS%29_is_greeted_by_Gabonese_Army_as_he_arrives_at_a_scheduled_meeting_with_General_Ntori_Longo%2C_chief_of_Gabonese_Armed_Services%2C_about.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/US_Navy_080107-N-4044H-049_Capt._John_B._Nowell_Jr.%2C_commodore%2C_Africa_Partnership_Station_%28APS%29_is_greeted_by_Gabonese_Army_as_he_arrives_at_a_scheduled_meeting_with_General_Ntori_Longo%2C_chief_of_Gabonese_Armed_Services%2C_about.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/US_Navy_080107-N-4044H-049_Capt._John_B._Nowell_Jr.%2C_commodore%2C_Africa_Partnership_Station_%28APS%29_is_greeted_by_Gabonese_Army_as_he_arrives_at_a_scheduled_meeting_with_General_Ntori_Longo%2C_chief_of_Gabonese_Armed_Services%2C_about.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2520" data-file-height="1800"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_080107-N-4044H-049_Capt._John_B._Nowell_Jr.,_commodore,_Africa_Partnership_Station_(APS)_is_greeted_by_Gabonese_Army_as_he_arrives_at_a_scheduled_meeting_with_General_Ntori_Longo,_chief_of_Gabonese_Armed_Services,_about.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>U.S. Navy Captain is greeted by Gabonese Army
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) and some of its specialized and related agencies, as well as of the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>; the <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a>; the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> (AU); the Central African Customs Union/Central African Economic and Monetary Community (UDEAC/CEMAC); EU/ACP association under the <a href="/wiki/Lom%C3%A9_Convention" title="Lomé Convention">Lomé Convention</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Communaute_Financiere_Africaine" class="mw-redirect" title="Communaute Financiere Africaine">Communaute Financiere Africaine</a> (CFA); the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Islamic_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of the Islamic Conference">Organization of the Islamic Conference</a> (OIC); the <a href="/wiki/Nonaligned_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonaligned Movement">Nonaligned Movement</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_Central_African_States" title="Economic Community of Central African States">Economic Community of Central African States</a> (ECCAS/CEEAC), among others. In 1995, Gabon withdrew from the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Petroleum_Exporting_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries">Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries</a> (OPEC), rejoining in 2016. Gabon was elected to a non-permanent seat on the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> for January 2010 through December 2011 and held the rotating presidency in March 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_Gabon" title="Armed Forces of Gabon">Armed Forces of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon has a small, professional <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_Gabon" title="Armed Forces of Gabon">military</a> of about 5,000 personnel, divided into <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Gabonese Army">army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Navy" title="Gabonese Navy">navy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Air_Force" title="Gabonese Air Force">air force</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">gendarmerie</a>, and police force. A 1,800-member guard provides security for the president.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="floatright">
       <div class="noresize" style="height: 320px; width: 300px; ">
        <map name="ImageMap_6af12da147022f1e"><area href="/wiki/Estuaire_Province" shape="rect" coords="53,82,92,115" alt="Estuaire Province" title="Estuaire Province"><area href="/wiki/Haut-Ogoou%C3%A9" shape="rect" coords="237,161,274,204" alt="Haut-Ogooué" title="Haut-Ogooué"><area href="/wiki/Moyen-Ogoou%C3%A9" shape="rect" coords="88,118,116,151" alt="Moyen-Ogooué" title="Moyen-Ogooué"><area href="/wiki/Ngouni%C3%A9" shape="rect" coords="105,176,144,218" alt="Ngounié" title="Ngounié"><area href="/wiki/Nyanga_Province" shape="rect" coords="109,248,136,284" alt="Nyanga Province" title="Nyanga Province"><area href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Ivindo" shape="rect" coords="192,65,218,99" alt="Ogooué-Ivindo" title="Ogooué-Ivindo"><area href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Lolo" shape="rect" coords="176,147,212,188" alt="Ogooué-Lolo" title="Ogooué-Lolo"><area href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Maritime" shape="rect" coords="29,167,67,218" alt="Ogooué-Maritime" title="Ogooué-Maritime"><area href="/wiki/Woleu-Ntem" shape="rect" coords="146,20,183,63" alt="Woleu-Ntem" title="Woleu-Ntem"></map>

        <div style="margin-left: 0px; margin-top: -20px; text-align: left;">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_provinces_numbered.png" title="About this image"><img alt="About this image" src="/w/extensions/ImageMap/resources/desc-20.png?15600" style="border: none;"></a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_Gabon" title="Subdivisions of Gabon">Subdivisions of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon is divided into nine provinces, which are further subdivided into 50 <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Gabon" title="Departments of Gabon">departments</a>. The president appoints the provincial governors, the prefects, and the subprefects.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The provinces are (capitals in parentheses): </p> 
      <ol>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Estuaire_Province" title="Estuaire Province">Estuaire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Libreville" title="Libreville">Libreville</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Haut-Ogoou%C3%A9_Province" title="Haut-Ogooué Province">Haut-Ogooué</a> (<a href="/wiki/Franceville" title="Franceville">Franceville</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Moyen-Ogoou%C3%A9_Province" title="Moyen-Ogooué Province">Moyen-Ogooué</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lambar%C3%A9n%C3%A9" title="Lambaréné">Lambaréné</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Ngouni%C3%A9_Province" title="Ngounié Province">Ngounié</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mouila" title="Mouila">Mouila</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Nyanga_Province" title="Nyanga Province">Nyanga</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tchibanga" title="Tchibanga">Tchibanga</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Ivindo_Province" title="Ogooué-Ivindo Province">Ogooué-Ivindo</a> (<a href="/wiki/Makokou" title="Makokou">Makokou</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Lolo_Province" title="Ogooué-Lolo Province">Ogooué-Lolo</a> (<a href="/wiki/Koulamoutou" title="Koulamoutou">Koulamoutou</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9-Maritime_Province" title="Ogooué-Maritime Province">Ogooué-Maritime</a> (<a href="/wiki/Port-Gentil" title="Port-Gentil">Port-Gentil</a>)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Woleu-Ntem_Province" title="Woleu-Ntem Province">Woleu-Ntem</a> (<a href="/wiki/Oyem" title="Oyem">Oyem</a>)</li>
      </ol> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Gabon" title="Geography of Gabon">Geography of Gabon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Gabon" title="Geology of Gabon">Geology of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_sat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Gabon_sat.png/220px-Gabon_sat.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Gabon_sat.png/330px-Gabon_sat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Gabon_sat.png/440px-Gabon_sat.png 2x" data-file-width="698" data-file-height="756"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_sat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Satellite image of Gabon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg/220px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg/440px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="850"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_GAB_present.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Gabon map of Köppen climate classification
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG/220px-Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG/330px-Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG/440px-Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Beach_scene_Libreville_5.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Beach scene in Gabon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon is located on the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> coast of central Africa on the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, between latitudes <a href="/wiki/3rd_parallel_north" title="3rd parallel north">3°N</a> and <a href="/wiki/4th_parallel_south" title="4th parallel south">4°S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/8th_meridian_east" title="8th meridian east">8°</a> and <a href="/wiki/15th_meridian_east" title="15th meridian east">15°E</a>. Gabon generally has an <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Equatorial climate">equatorial climate</a> with an extensive system of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">rainforests</a>, with 89.3% of its land area forested.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are three distinct regions: the coastal plains (ranging between 20 and 300&nbsp;km&nbsp;[10 and 190&nbsp;mi] from the ocean's shore), the mountains (the Cristal Mountains to the northeast of Libreville, the Chaillu Massif in the centre), and the <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> in the east. The coastal plains form a large section of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Equatorial_coastal_forests" title="Atlantic Equatorial coastal forests">Atlantic Equatorial coastal forests</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a> and contain patches of <a href="/wiki/Central_African_mangroves" title="Central African mangroves">Central African mangroves</a> especially on the <a href="/wiki/Muni_River" title="Muni River">Muni River</a> estuary on the border with <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-17">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Geologically, Gabon is primarily ancient <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleoproterozoic" title="Paleoproterozoic">Paleoproterozoic</a> igneous and metamorphic basement rock, belonging to the stable continental crust of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Craton" title="Congo Craton">Congo Craton</a>, a remnant section of extremely old continental crust. Some formations are more than two billion years old. Ancient rock units are overlain by marine <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>, lacustrine and continental sedimentary rocks as well as unconsolidated sediments and soils that formed in the last 2.5 million years of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a>. The rifting apart of the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> created rift basins that filled with sediments and formed the hydrocarbons which are now a keystone of the Gabonese economy.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Gabon is notable for the Oklo reactor zones, the only known <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactor</a> on Earth which was active two billion years ago. The site was discovered during uranium mining in the 1970s to supply the French nuclear power industry. </p>
      <p>Gabon's largest river is the <a href="/wiki/Ogoou%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Ogooué">Ogooué</a> which is 1,200 kilometres (750&nbsp;mi) long. Gabon has three <a href="/wiki/Karst_topography" class="mw-redirect" title="Karst topography">karst</a> areas where there are hundreds of caves located in the dolomite and limestone rocks. Some of the caves include Grotte du Lastoursville, Grotte du Lebamba, Grotte du Bongolo, and Grotte du Kessipougou. Many caves have not been explored yet. A National Geographic Expedition visited the caves in the summer of 2008 to document them.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gabon is also noted for efforts to preserve the natural environment. In 2002, President Omar Bongo Ondimba designated roughly 10% of the nation's territory to be part of its <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Gabon" title="List of national parks of Gabon">national park system</a> (with 13 parks in total), one of the largest proportions of <a href="/wiki/Nature_park" title="Nature park">nature parkland</a> in the world. The <a href="/wiki/National_Agency_for_National_Parks" title="National Agency for National Parks">National Agency for National Parks</a> manages Gabon's national park system. Gabon had a 2018 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 9.07/10, ranking it 9th globally out of 172 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Natural resources include petroleum, magnesium, iron, gold, uranium, and forests. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Gabon" title="Economy of Gabon">Economy of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg/220px-Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg/330px-Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg/440px-Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2670" data-file-height="2086"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gabon_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A proportional representation of Gabon's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon's economy is dominated by oil. <a href="/wiki/Oil_revenue" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil revenue">Oil revenues</a> constitute roughly 46% of the government's budget, 43% of the gross domestic product (GDP), and 81% of exports. Oil production is currently declining rapidly from its high point of 370,000 barrels per day in 1997. Some estimates suggest that Gabonese oil will be expended by 2025. In spite of the decreasing oil revenues, planning is only now beginning for an after-oil scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> The Grondin <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reservoir" title="Petroleum reservoir">Oil Field</a> was discovered in 50&nbsp;m (160&nbsp;ft) water depths 40&nbsp;km (25&nbsp;mi) offshore, in 1971 and produces from the Batanga <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maastrichtian" title="Maastrichtian">Maastrichtian</a> age forming an <a href="/wiki/Anticline" title="Anticline">anticline</a> salt <a href="/wiki/Structural_trap" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural trap">structural trap</a> which is about 2&nbsp;km (1.2&nbsp;mi) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gabonese public expenditures from the years of significant oil revenues were not spent efficiently. Overspending on the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Gabon_Railway" title="Trans-Gabon Railway">Trans-Gabon Railway</a>, the CFA franc devaluation of 1994, and periods of low oil prices caused serious debt problems that still plague the country.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gabon earned a poor reputation with the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Club" title="Paris Club">Paris Club</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) over the management of its debt and revenues. Successive IMF missions have criticized the government for overspending on off-budget items (in good years and bad), over-borrowing from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Bank">Central Bank</a>, and slipping on the schedule for <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatization</a> and administrative reform. However, in September 2005 Gabon successfully concluded a 15-month Stand-By Arrangement with the IMF. Another 3-year Stand-By Arrangement with the IMF was approved in May 2007. Because of the financial crisis and social developments surrounding the death of President Omar Bongo and the elections, Gabon was unable to meet its economic goals under the Stand-By Arrangement in 2009. Negotiations with the IMF were ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gabon's oil revenues have given it a per capita GDP of $8,600, unusually high for the region. However, a skewed income distribution and poor social indicators are evident.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The richest 20% of the population earn over 90% of the income while about a third of the Gabonese population lives in poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The economy is highly dependent on extraction, but primary materials are abundant. Before the discovery of oil, logging was the pillar of the Gabonese economy. Today, logging and manganese mining are the next-most-important income generators. Recent explorations suggest the presence of the world's largest unexploited iron ore deposit. For many who live in rural areas without access to employment opportunity in extractive industries, remittances from family members in urban areas or subsistence activities provide income.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Foreign and local observers have lamented the lack of diversity in the Gabonese economy. Various factors have so far limited the development of new industries: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>the market is small, about a million</li> 
       <li>dependent on imports from France</li> 
       <li>unable to capitalize on regional markets</li> 
       <li>entrepreneurial zeal not always present among the Gabonese</li> 
       <li>a fairly regular stream of oil "rent", even if it is diminishing</li>
      </ul> 
      <p>Further investment in the agricultural or <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Gabon" title="Tourism in Gabon">tourism</a> sectors is complicated by poor infrastructure. The small processing and service sectors that do exist are largely dominated by a few prominent local investors.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At World Bank and IMF insistence, the government embarked in the 1990s on a program of privatization of its state-owned companies and administrative reform, including reducing public sector employment and salary growth, but progress has been slow. The new government has voiced a commitment to work toward an economic transformation of the country but faces significant challenges to realize this goal.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Gabon" title="Demographics of Gabon">Demographics of Gabon</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px"> 
       <caption>
        Population in Gabon<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-3">[3]</a></sup> 
       </caption>

        <tr>

        </tr> 
        <tr>

        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG/220px-Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG/330px-Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG/440px-Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_on_Beach_in_Gabon.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Crowd on beach in Gabon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Gabon has a population of approximately 2.1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-3">[3]</a></sup> Historical and environmental factors caused Gabon's population to decline between 1900 and 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Gabon has one of the lowest <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population densities</a> of any country in Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> and the fourth highest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-7">[7]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Almost all Gabonese are of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> origin. Gabon has at least forty <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a> with differing languages and cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> Including <a href="/wiki/Fang_people" title="Fang people">Fang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myene_language" title="Myene language">Myènè</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punu_people" title="Punu people">Punu</a>-Échira, <a href="/wiki/Nzebi_languages" title="Nzebi languages">Nzebi</a>-Adouma, <a href="/wiki/Teke_people" title="Teke people">Teke</a>-Mbete, Mèmbè, <a href="/wiki/Kota_people_(Gabon)" title="Kota people (Gabon)">Kota</a>, Akélé.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> There are also various indigenous <a href="/wiki/Pygmy" class="mw-redirect" title="Pygmy">Pygmy</a> peoples: the <a href="/wiki/Bongo_people_(Gabon)" title="Bongo people (Gabon)">Bongo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baka_people_(Cameroon_and_Gabon)" title="Baka people (Cameroon and Gabon)">Baka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> The latter speak the only non-Bantu language in Gabon. More than 10,000 native French live in Gabon, including an estimated 2,000 dual nationals.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_10-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ethnic boundaries are less sharply drawn in Gabon than elsewhere in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Most ethnicities are spread throughout Gabon, leading to constant contact and interaction among the groups, and there is no ethnic tension. One important reason for this is that intermarriage is extremely common and every Gabonese person is connected by blood to many different tribes. Indeed, intermarriage is often required because among many tribes, marriage within the same tribe is prohibited because it is regarded as incest. This is because those tribes consist of the descendants of a specific ancestor, and therefore all members of the tribe are regarded as close kin to each other (identical to the clan system of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gotra" title="Gotra">Gotra</a> system in the Hindu caste system). French, the language of its former colonial ruler, is a unifying force. The Democratic Party of Gabon (PDG)'s historical dominance also has served to unite various ethnicities and local interests into a larger whole. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_centres">Population centres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gabon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Population centres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
